<div id="review_917150332" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/917150332" />
    <a title="Rebecca" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5875398-rebecca"><img alt="Rebecca" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1599578317p2/5875398.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/917150332?book_show_action=true">Apr 22, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Rebecca" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Rebecca" href="/user/show/5875398-rebecca">Rebecca</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5875398-rebecca?shelf=epistolary">epistolary</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5875398-rebecca?shelf=dystopian">dystopian</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5875398-rebecca?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5875398-rebecca?shelf=reviewed-third-way">reviewed-third-way</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5875398-rebecca?shelf=door-stoppers">door-stoppers</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5875398-rebecca?shelf=best-of-2014">best-of-2014</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer917150332" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13314297610080598069"><b>(4.5)</b> As beautiful as it is unsettling, this is a novel that will remain with you. Certainly there are aliens, but by no means can this book be relegated to the realms of sci-fi pulp. It is a very this-worldly story, blending a believable dystopian vision of Earth, commentary on cultural and religious imperialism, and a poignant portrait of a marriage under impossible strain.<br/><br/>Suburban London vicar Peter Leigh won the appointment of a lifetime: he will travel light years away to minister to the na</span>
  <span id="freeText13314297610080598069" style="display:none"><b>(4.5)</b> As beautiful as it is unsettling, this is a novel that will remain with you. Certainly there are aliens, but by no means can this book be relegated to the realms of sci-fi pulp. It is a very this-worldly story, blending a believable dystopian vision of Earth, commentary on cultural and religious imperialism, and a poignant portrait of a marriage under impossible strain.<br /><br />Suburban London vicar Peter Leigh won the appointment of a lifetime: he will travel light years away to minister to the natives of Oasis, a planet colonized by a shady American corporation. Meanwhile, on a mid-apocalyptic Earth plagued with natural disasters, his wife Bea is growing desperate. Peter’s religious platitudes are increasingly useless as the distance between them becomes emotional as well as physical. (The novel was written over the course of Faber’s wife’s final illness; she died in 2014. Knowing this adds an extra layer of ache to a tender scenario.)<br /><br />2014 was a big year for dystopian novels – <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1003807995" rel="nofollow noopener"><i>J</i> by</a> Howard Jacobson, <i>Station Eleven</i> by Emily St. John Mandel, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/866900971" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <i>California</i>
</a> by Edan Lepucki, even <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/840095878" rel="nofollow noopener"><i>Tigerman</i> </a>by Nick Harkaway) – but Faber’s has a very different aim (luckily, as I tend to rate pure dystopians quite low). Like Margaret Atwood, Faber is working to bring literary respectability to speculative fiction.<br /><br /><br />(Full review in February 2015 issue of <i>Third Way</i> magazine.)</span>
  <a data-text-id="13314297610080598069" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2277638540" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2277638540" />
    <a title="Ana" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1991298-ana"><img alt="Ana" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1528559847p2/1991298.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2277638540?book_show_action=true">Jun 10, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ana" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ana" href="/user/show/1991298-ana">Ana</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1991298-ana?shelf=read-in-2018">read-in-2018</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1991298-ana?shelf=own-and-read">own-and-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1991298-ana?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2277638540" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7497014434640352566">Science fiction? Religion? Aliens desperately wanting to hear the word of God? Are these the things you should know going into this book? <b>Or is it better to ignore all that and simply pick it up, turn page after page, and read the words of Michel Faber?</b><br/><br/>I am so glad I read this after <i>The Crimson Petal and the White</i>, because then I knew what an amazing writer Michel Faber is and that most certainly I won’t be feeling preached at. No matter where you fall on the spectrum of wanting or not wanting t</span>
  <span id="freeText7497014434640352566" style="display:none">Science fiction? Religion? Aliens desperately wanting to hear the word of God? Are these the things you should know going into this book? <b>Or is it better to ignore all that and simply pick it up, turn page after page, and read the words of Michel Faber?</b><br /><br />I am so glad I read this after <i>The Crimson Petal and the White</i>, because then I knew what an amazing writer Michel Faber is and that most certainly I won’t be feeling preached at. No matter where you fall on the spectrum of wanting or not wanting to read a novel that deals with Christianity and belief in space, there is something in <b>
  <i> The Book Of Strange New Things</i>
</b> that goes a bit further with the ‘missionary meets aliens’ trope, adding a couple of twists towards the end. <br /><br />It’s more of a literary novel than a science fiction work, and even though the action takes place in space, on C-2 or Oasis, thematically it deals with (planet)colonization and otherness (from a very Western perspective), love and loss, faith and compassion. <br /><br />Peter, the main character, former drug addict &amp; alcoholic turned Christian pastor, is sent to space by a dubious corporation called USIC to preach to the mysterious Oasans about the Bible, while leaving his wife, Bea, behind on Earth. I couldn’t help but see Peter’s religious dedication as another form of addiction, taken into consideration his relationship with his coworkers on the USIC compound and his letter exchanges with Bea. There is a detachment in the writer’s portrayal of Peter that leaves room for the reader to make his or her own assumptions about his character, and I appreciate that. <br /><br />One thing I found fascinating is the way Michel Faber centres the novel around cross-cultural communication, the idea of explaining our values and traditions to a completely different mindset, trying to translate religious imagery and its symbolism to people who have no idea what our world looks like in the first place. All the while without sounding preachy or polemic. Likewise, there is a palpable tension that builds up once you realize that there is a bigger picture, involving the fate of humanity, but I’m not going to spoil that by giving away the plot.<br /><br />Another thing that I appreciated is how realistically the author depicts a relationship - one such as Peter and Bea’s - that is being tested to the very limits. They are both flawed characters, sincere in their belief yet not immune to doubt. I loved Bea, even though we only see her in the beginning and through the letters, and I found Peter to be a dreamer, albeit not a very likeable one. <br /><br />I’m not sure <b>
  <i>The Book Of Strange New Things</i>
</b> is the kind of book you wholeheartedly love, but I think it’s remarkable how Faber manages to make something alien, like the Oasans, seem more familiar than the USIC crew members, and how something familiar, like the stories of the Bible, sounds so very strange when limited by language. The fact that there won’t be a sequel makes it even more intriguing and it will most definitely get a reaction from you. Those who’ve read it know what I’m talking about.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7497014434640352566" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1577344574" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1577344574" />
    <a title="Hanneke" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/17944903-hanneke"><img alt="Hanneke" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1546964664p2/17944903.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1577344574?book_show_action=true">Mar 12, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Hanneke" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Hanneke" href="/user/show/17944903-hanneke">Hanneke</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781782114086" href="/book/show/26020767-the-book-of-strange-new-things">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1577344574" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10606819625479740379">Who would ever have imagined that I would be fully submerged in a scifi novel whose protagonist is a missionary transported to a planet at a distance of a trillion miles from earth! What is even more remarkable is that a pastor had been urgently requested by the native people of the planet, thus not by the scientists and engineers who are establishing a base for future human habitation. 'Native people' is, of course, a wrong denomination, we could use 'aliens', but who are the aliens in this pla</span>
  <span id="freeText10606819625479740379" style="display:none">Who would ever have imagined that I would be fully submerged in a scifi novel whose protagonist is a missionary transported to a planet at a distance of a trillion miles from earth! What is even more remarkable is that a pastor had been urgently requested by the native people of the planet, thus not by the scientists and engineers who are establishing a base for future human habitation. 'Native people' is, of course, a wrong denomination, we could use 'aliens', but who are the aliens in this place?<br />I found it a fascinating and unsettling book. Faber's story unfolds slowly. Pastor Peter is bursting with goodwill and sacrifice and does not allow himself to despair in feelings of alienation and misunderstanding. Being a converted drug addict and alcoholist, he has just the exact patient nature of the true believer that is needed here, optimistic and shortsighted, you could even say deliberate and nauseously innocent. Radical converts find that a comfortable state of mind. He wants to love his flock, even though he cannot distinguish between the Oasans individually; they all seem to be the same like in a beehive. Peter can only recognise them by the colour of their robes, so he names them by number to which he attaches the colour of their robes. He is at a loss where their eyes or mouths are. Their language sounds like of a 'field of brittle reeds and rain-sodden lettuces being cleared by a machete.' They cannot pronounce the T's and S's in English, but they do understand the Biblical language and love to listen to it. <br />The human compound is inhabited by people who are chosen by UNIC for their unemotional attitudes, even for extreme boringness. Pastor Peter is, consequently, the most emotional person around and viewed as a person who causes unnecessary upheaval. The humans accept this, as he is instrumental to their well-being vis-a-vis the Oasans.<br />This novel cannot be classified as a scifi book in the conventional way. It is the first one I read where a human tries to be in a close relationship with aliens. An utter failure, but touching to read. Goodwill from both sides cannot bridge the gap which is impossible to cross. The fact that Pastor Peter actually believes so intensely is more exotic to me than the behaviour of the aliens. The aliens obviously gained strength and were comforted to hear the stories from the Book of Strange New Things. That is touching, but what does it mean. There is actually no message. <br /><br />Special book! A story like no other. Faber writes in a detached way, but you still get pulled in and feel attached. Loved it.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10606819625479740379" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1028988843" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1028988843" />
    <a title="Judy" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3333943-judy"><img alt="Judy" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1267037102p2/3333943.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1028988843?book_show_action=true">Aug 17, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Judy" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Judy" href="/user/show/3333943-judy">Judy</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3333943-judy?shelf=21st-century-fiction">21st-century-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3333943-judy?shelf=speculative-fiction">speculative-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3333943-judy?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1028988843" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2653116250802405025"> Michel Faber's new novel, due to be released on October 28, is itself a book of strange new things. I remember devouring his previous novel, <i>The Crimson Petal and the White</i>. It was historical fiction about a prostitute, a bestseller, mildly trashy but with good writing and a fabulous heroine. <i>The Book of Strange New Things</i> is not any of that.<br/><br/>Peter is a recovering alcoholic and drug user who became a minister after he met and married Beatrice, who nursed him through his final overdose. He is dee</span>
  <span id="freeText2653116250802405025" style="display:none"> Michel Faber's new novel, due to be released on October 28, is itself a book of strange new things. I remember devouring his previous novel, <i>The Crimson Petal and the White</i>. It was historical fiction about a prostitute, a bestseller, mildly trashy but with good writing and a fabulous heroine. <i>The Book of Strange New Things</i> is not any of that.<br /><br />Peter is a recovering alcoholic and drug user who became a minister after he met and married Beatrice, who nursed him through his final overdose. He is deeply committed to his faith in the way that people are when they give up past bad habits and need something new to hang on to.<br /><br />Bea is the practical member of the couple, but deeply devoted to keeping Peter happy and sober. When he gets recruited by USIC, a multinational corporation, to go to a planet light years away and serve as Chaplain to its indigenous creatures, Bea is not chosen to go with him. Quite soon, I figured out that they chose Peter because of his wide-eyed gullibility.<br /><br />So he goes to the planet Oasis, pretty much goes native in short order, and feels more at home with the nonhuman Oasans than with the earthlings. The Book of Strange New Things is the Oasans' name for the Bible. <br /><br />Faber does an excellent job of creating Peter's character, his obsession with the Oasans and getting the gospel to them, and his obliviousness to other humans. Every other person on Oasis has something odd about them, some troubled past, but none are looking for religion.<br /><br />By page 175, I could tell that something was very wrong on Oasis and settled in to the remaining 300 plus pages to find out what that something was. The trouble for me was that it turned out not to be a sci fi thriller/first contact story but rather Peter's true redemption story. <br /><br />He is able to communicate with Bea by a sort of interplanetary text messaging device. Like many men who travel for work, my husband included, he is not good at long distance communication. Add to this his extreme self-absorption and marital discord develops.<br /><br />Religion in science fiction or speculative fiction is somewhat rare and is sometimes done extremely well, as in Mary Doria Russel's <i>The Sparrow</i>. You would think it would be used more often. Michel Faber handles the religion aspect well, also the tension in Peter and Bea's marriage, and the beginnings on Earth of an apocalyptic stew of climate change, end of oil, and economic breakdown. He also creates a convincing scenario of USIC, obviously exploiting a new planet for scientific and commercial gain.<br /><br />In the end though, the novel is just a story about a loser who finally grows up and begins to get a grip. The creepy menace on Oasis, the true reason for the native Oasans' desire for Christianity, and the fate of both planets all just peter out (no pun intended) and it is all about Peter, as it has been for almost 500 pages.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2653116250802405025" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1125950264" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1125950264" />
    <a title="Thomas" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2018505-thomas"><img alt="Thomas" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1567953607p2/2018505.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1125950264?book_show_action=true">Dec 07, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Thomas" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Thomas" href="/user/show/2018505-thomas">Thomas</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2018505-thomas?shelf=read-on-kindle">read-on-kindle</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2018505-thomas?shelf=adult-fiction">adult-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2018505-thomas?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2018505-thomas?shelf=own-electronic">own-electronic</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1125950264" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11991490119790094197">What the heck did I just read? As I write this review I still do not know how to answer that question. The plot of <i>The Book of Strange New Things</i> centers on Peter Leigh, a man of faith who embarks on a mission to evangelize a group of aliens he calls the Oasans. Meanwhile, his wife on earth, Beatrice, suffers as their home planet collapses due to natural disasters. Peter drifts farther away from Bea the closer he gets to the Oasans. He soon must decide who or what matters most to him: his wife o</span>
  <span id="freeText11991490119790094197" style="display:none">What the heck did I just read? As I write this review I still do not know how to answer that question. The plot of <i>The Book of Strange New Things</i> centers on Peter Leigh, a man of faith who embarks on a mission to evangelize a group of aliens he calls the Oasans. Meanwhile, his wife on earth, Beatrice, suffers as their home planet collapses due to natural disasters. Peter drifts farther away from Bea the closer he gets to the Oasans. He soon must decide who or what matters most to him: his wife or his mission.<br /><br />The lack of conflict throughout this novel acts as its first and foremost weakness. For the first 60% of the book, Peter travels to space, meets his fellow adventurers, thinks about religion, and embraces an amiable group of aliens. Michael Faber institutes no impetus for further reading, unless someone by chance enjoys Peter's lukewarm introspection and too-tame journey. Even at the 60% mark the clash between Peter and Bea about their relationship feels forced, in that the epistolary format of Bea's communication detracts from the immediacy of her situation. I struggle to write about what else happens in <i>The Book of Strange New Things</i>, because even though the plot does develop and novel events do occur, every detail felt so non-urgent and inessential that I ended up finishing the book just for the sake of finishing it.<br /><br />The lack of character development also weakened this book's appeal. Peter tells us that he has a difficult life before he discovered faith and that Bea grew up in an abusive household. However, none of this past struggle contributes to these characters' developmental arcs throughout the story and they thus read as rather simple folk. The static nature of the Oasans also felt like a missed opportunity. Faber does not at all give depth to the science-fiction facets of this story, nor does he make anything about the Oasans remarkable other than their ugly appearances. The dearth of interesting events and intriguing characters contributes to my overall lack of connection with this story and any of its inherent meaning.<br /><br />The Goodreads blurb for this book states that Peter and Bea's struggle "lay bare a profound meditation on faith, love tested beyond endurance, and our responsibility to those closest to us." I would argue that while Michael Faber includes all of these topics in his story, the book's glaring flaws obscure any "profound meditation." The only aspect of the book I found compelling centered on Peter's use of religion as a coping mechanism ad the way he deludes himself and loses his grasp of humankind because of the Oasans. Perhaps I just missed the point of the book because of its suffocating focus on religion that still did not provide any thought-provoking questions or insights. I would only recommend this book to those who feel extremely, intensely drawn in by its synopsis.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11991490119790094197" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2841549289" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2841549289" />
    <a title="Rachel (TheShadesofOrange)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7487619-rachel-theshadesoforange"><img alt="Rachel (TheShadesofOrange)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1519620140p2/7487619.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2841549289?book_show_action=true">Mar 04, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Rachel (TheShadesofOrange)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Rachel (TheShadesofOrange)" href="/user/show/7487619-rachel-theshadesoforange">Rachel (TheShadesofOrange)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7487619-rachel-theshadesoforange?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2841549289" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12801756204333316146"><b>4.0 Stars<br/>Sometimes I read a book at exactly the right moment. This was one of those times. </b><br/><br/>I absolutely loved the ideas behind this novel. While this is technically about alien contact, this was really a story about relationships. The novel explored how these relationships can become strained over emotional & physical distances. As a married woman, this story hit home for me in so many personal ways. The tone of the story was more sad than I expected, which unfortunately did slightly dampen my r</span>
  <span id="freeText12801756204333316146" style="display:none"><b>4.0 Stars<br />Sometimes I read a book at exactly the right moment. This was one of those times. </b><br /><br />I absolutely loved the ideas behind this novel. While this is technically about alien contact, this was really a story about relationships. The novel explored how these relationships can become strained over emotional &amp; physical distances. As a married woman, this story hit home for me in so many personal ways. The tone of the story was more sad than I expected, which unfortunately did slightly dampen my reading experience. <br /><br />While technically considered science fiction, this book will more appeal to literary readers like hardcore scifi fans. Certainly, there was an exploration of the strange new alien species,  but the themes of the story were more reflective of what it means to be human.<br /><br />While this was certainly not Christian fiction, there was a lot of discussion of religion, spirituality and faith. You do not have to be religious in order to enjoy this one, but you do need to be okay with reading about the subject matter. I appreciated that the main character and his wife were not stereotypical evangelists, but instead seemed to be fairly grounded, non judgmental believers.<br /><br />I previously read and loved the author’s other book, Under the Skin, and while I ended up loving both novels, they were very, very different. If you like one of these novels, you will not necessarily like the other.  Thankfully, they both happened to work for me. I have definitely become a fan of Michel Faber, who is a very skilled writer and storyteller. The characters in this novel were so well drawn and truly felt like real people. I don’t always get along with literary fiction, but Faber’s style just works for me. <br /><br />Admittedly, on this initial read, I did feel that some of the middle sections of the book dragged a bit, but I might feel differently upon a reread. The ending wasn't quite what I wanted, but it was still a strong novel. <br /><br />Overall, I loved a lot of aspects of this novel and would recommend it to anyone looking for a bittersweet piece of literary science fiction that focuses on the trials of maintaining a long term relationship. </span>
  <a data-text-id="12801756204333316146" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer989491295" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6191862398842947261"><br/>This book took me a while to get through, I ended up reading it really slowly because I didn't want it to end. It was so completely engrossing.<b> I wanted to savour every page ,while trying to fend off my desire to devour this novel.</b><br/><br/>The cover is exquisite and admittedly what drew me to this book, however it was the impeccable writing and beautiful imagery that kept me reading. I loved the world of Oasis, and this may sound clichéd but I honestly felt like I was right there when I was reading. I wa</span>
  <span id="freeText6191862398842947261" style="display:none"><br />This book took me a while to get through, I ended up reading it really slowly because I didn't want it to end. It was so completely engrossing.<b> I wanted to savour every page ,while trying to fend off my desire to devour this novel.</b><br /><br />The cover is exquisite and admittedly what drew me to this book, however it was the impeccable writing and beautiful imagery that kept me reading. I loved the world of Oasis, and this may sound clichéd but I honestly felt like I was right there when I was reading. I was completely transported. This would make a great bookclub read for a wide variety of readers.<br /><br /><br />Note: I received this book for free in exchange for an honest review.<br /><br />P.S- This title is currently available for request on Netgalley <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.netgalley.com/catalog/show/id/45614">https://www.netgalley.com/catalog/sho...</a><br /></span>

//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('muh6HYR82K+U2Kj5s1mU0y03vHIDKKWGoP9cRX2bUE4S2L/NH/D5ezDLJExY5bUPAnH9NElgrF2/FezPzs/s/A==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('vH/CC+vW5IxxCsc9mKmJziUD+aU/NLOYXdipWwf0ysw0TwfbcFrFWNUZS4hzFagSCkW443V8ukNCMhnRtKB2fg==')})
  }
//]]>
</script>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer4933275873818695186">Michel Faber (born 13 April 1960) is a Dutch writer of English-language fiction.<br/><br/>Faber was born in The Hague, The Netherlands. He and his parents emigrated to Australia in 1967. He attended primary and secondary school in the Melbourne suburbs of Boronia and Bayswater, then attended the University Of Melbourne, studying Dutch, Philosophy, Rhetoric, English Language (a course involving translation a</span>
  <span id="freeText4933275873818695186" style="display:none">Michel Faber (born 13 April 1960) is a Dutch writer of English-language fiction.<br /><br />Faber was born in The Hague, The Netherlands. He and his parents emigrated to Australia in 1967. He attended primary and secondary school in the Melbourne suburbs of Boronia and Bayswater, then attended the University Of Melbourne, studying Dutch, Philosophy, Rhetoric, English Language (a course involving translation and criticism of Anglo-Saxon and Middle English texts) and English Literature. He graduated in 1980. He worked as a cleaner and at various other casual jobs, before training as a nurse at Marrickville and Western Suburbs hospitals in Sydney. He nursed until the mid-1990s. In 1993 he, his second wife and family emigrated to Scotland, where they still reside.</span>

        script.src = load[i];
        first.parentNode.insertBefore(script, first);
      }
    }
  if (w.attachEvent) {
    w.attachEvent('onload', go);
  } else {
    w.addEventListener('load', go, false);
  }
}(window, document,
    ['//assets.pinterest.com/js/pinit.js',
     '//apis.google.com/js/plusone.js'
    ]
  ));

//]]>
</script>

      </div>

    </div>
